## Abstract
With the increasing application of GenAI in education, researchers and practitioners are paying more and more attention to its effectiveness and impact in teaching and learning. This can be evidenced by the increasing number of literature reviews on this topic published in recent years. However, these literature reviews seldom analysed the effectiveness of GenAI-powered educational applications from the human–AI interaction perspective, which is a widely recognized critical factor influencing the effectiveness of such GenAI-powered educational applications. In response, this study systematically reviews 56 empirical studies on the application of GenAI in education. To explicitly address this gap from the human–AI interaction perspective, we analysed the reviewed studies using the AIED-HCD framework, which conceptualizes three human–AI interaction modes along the dimensions of human control and AI automation, and examined educational contextual factors and educational tasks supported by GenAI to assess how interaction modes vary across teaching and learning contexts. In addition, we conducted a sensitivity analysis to evaluate the robustness of findings across these modes. We demonstrated that, although current educational practices remain cautious towards interaction modes with a high level of AI automation, the mode characterized by both high human control and high AI automation has begun to emerge as a trend, demonstrating promising potential for integrating the respective strengths of humans and AI. Furthermore, sensitivity analysis reveals that many studies lack sufficient detail in their statistical reporting, and the reported effect sizes often fall below the thresholds required for acceptable statistical power. Based on these findings, we recommend: (i) beyond exploring how to improve the practical use of AI automation under human supervision and control, educational researchers and practitioners should carefully choose and implement suitable human–AI interaction settings according to the specific context of use, with higher levels of AI automation applied only when supported by appropriate task design and pedagogical guidance; (ii) researchers should improve methodological transparency by estimating appropriate sample sizes and testing assumptions to ensure the reliability of empirical findings.
### Practitioner notes
## What is already known about this topic
\* Generative artificial intelligence can efficiently analyse vast amounts of textual information and perform complex natural language processing and generation tasks, demonstrating powerful language intelligence capabilities.
\* Generative artificial intelligence is increasingly being integrated into various educational systems, and with its exceptional capabilities, its potential to support educational applications is gradually being explored and put into practice.
## What this paper adds
\* Based on the AIED-HCD conceptual framework, this study systematically classifies current GenAI-based empirical research by examining two key dimensions, namely the extent to which human control and automation through GenAI are enabled, and offers a holistic perspective on the current state of research.
\* A sensitivity analysis was conducted to examine whether the empirical evidence reported in current GenAI-based studies demonstrates sufficient statistical power to support future research and practical implementation.
## Implications for practice and/or policy
\* Stay continuously informed about the latest advancements in AI technologies and carefully verify their suitability for different interaction modes to enhance educational user experiences and deliver systematically measurable improvements and efficiencies to the educational landscape.
\* Current GenAI-based empirical studies should include more detailed statistical reporting, such as whether assumption tests were conducted and the specific results of effect sizes, to support empirical evidence and enhance transparency.
## INTRODUCTION
In recent years, a growing number of GenAI-powered educational systems and tools have been developed and adopted to support teaching and learning. Likewise, an increasing number of students are leveraging GenAI to complete various academic tasks (Playfoot et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0103)), such as assisting with introductory programming exercises (Wermelinger, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0126)) or serving as a writing assistant for essay assignments (Malik et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0079)). Concurrently, educators are actively exploring and evaluating the pedagogical potential of GenAI. For instance, they are employing GenAI to develop high-quality learning resources (Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034)), design assessment tasks (Kasneci et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0048)) and generate feedback (Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)). Across these applications, prior studies have primarily focused on improving the effectiveness of teaching and learning, understanding as the positive influence of GenAI on learning and teaching processes in educational contexts (Gökoğlu & Erdoğdu, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0035); Li et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0070); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)) and enhancing the professional productivity (Dempere et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0023); Law, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0062)). Collectively, the integration of GenAI by both students and educators for a variety of educational purposes underscores its growing prominence and transformative potential within the educational landscape. Notably, these practices reflect diverse modes of human–AI interaction embedded in GenAI-powered educational systems, which merit further investigation and synthesis.
With the growing prevalence of GenAI, researchers have sought to develop a general understanding of its effectiveness and impact in supporting teaching and learning, often through systematic literature reviews (Ansari et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0004); Baidoo-Anu & Ansah, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0008); Bhullar et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0012); Dempere et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0023)). In the early stages of GenAI's emergence, researchers' efforts primarily concentrated on its technological developments, potential benefits and associated challenges within educational contexts, utilizing methodologies such as systematic reviews and bibliometric analyses (Bhullar et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0012); Dempere et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0023); Karthikeyan, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0047); Law, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0062)). These studies highlighted critical issues, including the practical feasibility and ethical implications of GenAI-based innovations in real-world educational settings (Karthikeyan, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0047); Rahman & Watanobe, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0105); Yan, Sha, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0133)), and proposed directions for future research (Baidoo-Anu & Ansah, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0008); Bannister et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0009); Guo, Zheng, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0039); Law, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0062)). Suggested avenues include assessing the differential effects of GenAI on learning outcomes across diverse student populations (Bannister et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0009); Bhullar et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0012); de la Torre & Baldeon-Calisto, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0022); Dempere et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0023); Samala et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0106); Vargas-Murillo et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0122)), exploring effective implementation strategies to realize GenAI's educational potential (Ansari et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0004); Montenegro-Rueda et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0086); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)) and investigating educators' and institutions' attitudes towards its adoption (Nikolic et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0092)). Collectively, these efforts contribute valuable insights towards the broader integration of GenAI in education. However, most of the reviewed studies did not focus exclusively on empirical investigations evaluating GenAI's effectiveness and educational impact through data collected from real-world teachers and students. Although some existing literature reviews (Belkina et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0011); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)) have focused on empirical studies and synthesized cases of GenAI applications in education, these studies still have several limitations. For example, current studies tend to examine GenAI from specific perspectives, such as its practical adoption and its effectiveness in improving learning outcomes in higher education (Belkina et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0011)), its impact on student engagement (Lo et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0078)) or its potential and challenges in areas including learning support, teaching support, feedback and assessment (Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)). However, this body of work is characterized by fragmented coverage and constrained analytical scopes, which means that it provides only partial evidence for understanding how GenAI is used and with what educational impact across different contexts. In addition, some reviews have approached the integration of AI in education from the perspective of hybrid intelligent systems, aiming to explore how human and AI can be more effectively combined. For instance, certain studies have investigated the potential of ontology to bridge the gap between human and AI (Pileggi, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0102)), while others have examined how hybrid intelligent approaches can optimize specific educational tasks, such as university examination timetabling problems (Ghaffar et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0033)). However, existing research has not yet provided a comprehensive understanding of how GenAI interacts with humans across different educational contexts, thereby limiting our comprehension of its overall educational value.
Against this backdrop, it is necessary to use new analytical perspectives to analyses and summaries existing literature to uncover new insights. Educational theory and empirical studies have long established that interaction is a core component of the teaching and learning process (Lee et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0063)). Whether it involves teacher–student interaction, peer collaboration or exchanges between learners and learning resources, interaction plays a critical role in fostering meaning-making and enhancing educational outcomes (Dang et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0019); Lee et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0063)). With the rapid development of GenAI, educational settings have inevitably incorporated human–AI interactions (Sun, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0116)). For example, in a GenAI-powered feedback system, feedback may be provided through a chatbot, enabling students to discuss the feedback and clarify their confusion through interaction with the bot. In contrast, other systems may allow teachers to review the automatically generated feedback or directly prompt GenAI to generate feedback on their behalf and send it to students. These varying modes of human–AI interaction can influence how students engage with the feedback and, ultimately, impact learning outcomes (Kazemitabaar, Hou, et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0052)). These interactions affect not only student learning processes but also teachers' instructional practices (Cui, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0017); Sun, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0116)).
Given that the effectiveness and impact of GenAI in educational contexts inherently depend on how humans interact with and respond to AI tools, neglecting the human–AI interaction dimension may introduce substantial uncertainty into research findings, potentially leading to inaccurate or misleading conclusions (Bach et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0007); Floridi et al., [2018](#bjet70055-bib-0031); Raees et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0104)). Importantly, this concern aligns with Cukurova's conceptual framework on human–AI interaction, which suggests that over-reliance on AI for cognitively demanding tasks may undermine the long-term development of critical competencies (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)). Crucially, from Cukurova's perspective (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)), this risk arises not from AI itself, but from how responsibility for meaning-making and critical decision-making is shared between humans and AI in educational contexts. However, current research provides insufficient attention to the critical dimension of human–AI interaction, and there remains a lack of systematic and in-depth exploration in this area. Existing studies on the application of GenAI in education often vaguely describe the human–AI interaction process in GenAI-empowered educational systems (Guo, Zheng, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0039); Nikolic et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0092); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)), limiting their ability to accurately reveal GenAI's actual effectiveness and its applicability across different educational settings. To address this gap, it is essential to draw on emerging conceptual frameworks of human–AI interaction to conduct an in-depth analysis of interaction processes within existing GenAI-driven educational tools and systems, thereby exploring their potential educational value.
Beyond the conceptual limitation, another limitation of current research lies in the insufficient attention to the reliability of empirical findings. Most existing studies tended to remain at a descriptive level or focus on highlighting the positive effects of GenAI tools (Deng et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0024)), while few rigorously examined whether these reported effects were supported by sufficient statistical power. The absence of such analyses inevitably could undermine the credibility of research conclusions, making the reported insights into the educational impacts of GenAI potentially less reliable and, in some cases, susceptible to statistical limitations (Yan et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0134); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)). In certain situations, small sample sizes or insufficient statistical power could even lead to misleading conclusions about effectiveness (Weidlich et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0125)). This gap underscores the need for more rigorous evaluations of statistical validity within each human–AI interaction mode to ensure that conclusions regarding the effectiveness of GenAI in education are both trustworthy and meaningful. To address this research gap, we conducted a systematic literature review following the PRISMA guidelines (Page et al., [2021](#bjet70055-bib-0096)). Building on established definitions of human–AI interaction proposed in prior research (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018); Gomez et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0036); Herrmann, [2022](#bjet70055-bib-0040); Li et al., [2022](#bjet70055-bib-0068); Schleidgen et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0109)), we adopted Cukurova's AIED-HCD conceptual framework (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)). Grounded in theoretical and empirical work on learning and cognition, this framework characterized different ways in which AI participated in educational activity along the dimensions of human control and AI automation and elucidated how different configurations reshaped the organization of learning opportunities and influenced the development of human competencies over time. Prior research emphasized that human–AI interaction modes cannot be meaningfully interpreted when detached from their pedagogical contexts and cautioned that the uncritical delegation of cognitively demanding educational tasks to AI may undermine the long-term development of critical human competencies (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)). From this perspective, Cukurova framed AI systems as elements of broader socio-technical learning ecosystems, arguing that human–AI interactions therefore need to be intentionally shaped through instructional design rather than left to emerge from unstructured use (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)). Accordingly, the pedagogical significance of different human–AI interaction modes could not be inferred from interaction configurations alone but had to be examined in relation to the learning tasks, stages of learning and instructional designs in which they were embedded.
Building on the AIED-HCD conceptual framework (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)), the present review did not stop at identifying human–AI interaction modes, but further examined how these modes were embedded in concrete educational contexts across existing empirical studies. To this end, the review adopted a pedagogical embedding perspective to analyse relevant studies, with particular attention to the purposes for which GenAI was applied, the types of educational tasks it supported, the educational levels involved, the roles of key stakeholders and the experimental designs employed. By uncovering the contextual characteristics commonly associated with specific human–AI interaction modes, we aim to provide deeper insights into which factors are most closely related to the use and effectiveness of GenAI-driven applications. This analytical strategy not only reveals the current trends in the development of different interaction modes but also highlights the concrete ways in which GenAI is implemented in authentic educational contexts, thereby offering new perspectives on its overall effectiveness and educational impact. After that, to gain a deeper and more quantitative understanding of the effectiveness of GenAI-driven educational applications, we conducted a sensitivity analysis for each category of empirical studies within specific human–AI interaction modes. Sensitivity analysis is widely recommended in meta-analytic research (Vaccaro et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0120)) and is useful for examining the robustness and validity of empirical findings.
This study contributes to the existing research by systematically synthesizing empirical evidence on GenAI's educational effectiveness through the underexplored lens of human–AI interaction. By thoroughly examining how different modes of human–AI interaction influence current educational practices, our study addresses research gaps in current GenAI literature and provides evidence-based guidance for effective implementation of GenAI in educational practice.
## BACKGROUND
### Conceptualizing human–AI interaction in education
To identify current modes of human–AI interaction in educational settings, Cukurova ([2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)) proposed a two-dimensional framework that positions and conceptualizes AI along the axes of Human Control and AI Automation. The framework is not intended merely to classify different interaction modes, but to offer a principled way of conceptualizing how AI is involved in educational activity and instructional systems, and thus is adopted in our work to guide the analysis. Within this framework, human control refers to the degree of autonomy, decision-making authority and level of involvement that users have when interacting with AI systems (Alfredo et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0002)). It is not solely concerned with whether users are capable of operating the system, but with whether they can actively guide the definition of instructional objectives, the design of learning pathways and the interpretation of outcomes. When human control is high, critical decisions are made by humans, while AI primarily provides information, recommendations or visualizations that support reflection and improvement in teaching and learning processes. Conversely, when users passively receive information without the ability to make independent decisions, human control is regarded as low. AI automation, by contrast, measures the degree to which a system performs tasks traditionally carried out by humans, including data collection, pattern recognition, decision-making and the implementation of instructional interventions. Systems with high levels of AI automation are capable of independently analysing data, generating predictions and directly delivering instructional content, assessment results or intervention strategies to users. In contrast, low-automation systems primarily serve as cognitive aids that assist human thought processes without acting independently. Accordingly, the appropriateness of automation is contingent on the specific learning task and context, and higher levels of automation do not inherently result in more effective pedagogical outcomes. In particular, uncritical delegation of learning-related tasks to AI may undermine learners' long-term development of critical competencies (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)).
From a theoretical perspective, the AIED-HCD framework articulates two analytic dimensions, human control and AI automation, that structure different modes of human–AI interaction in educational settings. While the framework could be useful for classifying different forms of human–AI interaction, its contribution goes beyond classification, as it is grounded in a theoretical argument concerning how AI participates in learning processes. Within this argument, AI is conceptualized as a means of externalizing, internalizing and extending human cognition, rather than simply as an applied educational tool. Through these conceptualizations, the framework explicitly links patterns of human–AI interaction to how human competence development is supported, emphasizing that learning outcomes depend on how cognitive responsibility is distributed between humans and AI. Figure [1](#bjet70055-fig-0001) (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)) is included to assist readers in understanding the theoretical positioning of the three human AI interaction modes within the human control by AI automation space and to serve as a theoretical anchor for the subsequent analysis.
[![](/cms/asset/52885309-4ce7-43b1-841b-176678f9ed2c/bjet70055-fig-0001-m.png)](/cms/asset/ed6bd353-489b-4655-b9e5-2e3d7aba8ab9/bjet70055-fig-0001-m.jpg)
\*\*FIGURE 1\*\*
[Open in figure viewer](#)[PowerPoint](/action/downloadFigures?id=bjet70055-fig-0001&partId=&doi=10.1111%2Fbjet.70055)
The AIED-HCD conceptual framework proposed by Cukurova ([2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)).
#### Low human control and high AI automation (Externalization)
This mode, termed ‘Externalization’, describes the process of transferring human tasks and cognitive functions to an external AI system for execution. AI is viewed as an execution tool, using high-level automation to complete or optimize tasks such as automated grading, providing automated feedback to learners or analysing performance data to support diagnostics that were originally handled by humans. A typical example is Intelligent Tutoring Systems (ITS). These systems partially replace teachers' instruction and grading tasks by providing cognitive diagnostics and adaptive feedback, enabling large-scale personalized learning (Kawamura et al., [2021](#bjet70055-bib-0050)). Other examples include the use of AI-generated synthetic instructional videos to replace traditional human-recorded lectures, thereby reducing teachers' instructional workload to some extent (Leiker et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0066)) as well as the adoption of AI-driven automated grading systems to assist teachers in completing assignment marking and evaluation tasks (Liu, Chatain, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0074)). Studies (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)) show no significant difference in learning outcomes or experiences. Both examples illustrate a clear ‘Externalization’ of traditional teaching duties to AI.
#### High human control and low AI automation (Internalization)
This mode, termed ‘Internalization’, focuses on scenarios where AI provides external, data-driven insights (eg, via visualizations and dashboards) that prompt humans to internalize this information, leading to deep reflection and adjustments in their strategies. The final decision-making and meaning-making remain with humans. Crucially, as humans gradually succeed in internalizing these reflective skills and enhancing their competence, the role of the AI model will progressively fade. For instance, multimodal learning analytics systems can visualize group speaking time, interaction types and other behavioural data, feeding it back to students and teachers to foster self-regulation and collaborative optimization (Aslan et al., [2019](#bjet70055-bib-0005)). It is worth noting that not all learning analytics fall within the scope of AI. In this context, however, we refer specifically to AI-powered learning analytics approach.
#### High human control and high AI automation (Hybrid Intelligence)
This mode, termed ‘Hybrid Intelligence’, represents a symbiotic partnership where human and AI capabilities are deeply integrated. It leverages strong algorithmic support while preserving human leadership, enabling a dynamic blend of human and AI system collaboration. In tightly coupled human–AI systems, both AI and humans contribute their strengths, engaging in real-time interaction to jointly extend human cognition and decision-making capacity. The resulting hybrid intelligence is greater than the sum of its parts. Through deepening interactions, both entities continually shape and enhance each other's capabilities. An example is the exploration of how humans collaborating with AI can enhance students' self-regulated learning through the introduction of the ‘trigger’ concept and a hybrid model of human–AI shared regulation (Järvelä et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0045)). Alternatively, teachers can collaborate with AI in designing course materials to support their teaching tasks (Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034)). This kind of human–AI interaction not only accomplishes tasks but also fosters the expansion of human capabilities.
Beyond these three modes, the framework's two dimensions also define a fourth quadrant, characterized by low human control and low AI automation. This quadrant largely corresponds to traditional educational technologies, such as digital content repositories or simple drill-and-practice software. These systems neither provide intelligent automation nor involve generative or adaptive AI capabilities, and thus do not constitute meaningful forms of human–AI interaction. As these technologies fall outside the scope of our review, this fourth mode will not be discussed further.
This AIED-HCD framework is not only an empirical generalization of interaction modes between humans and AI but also a theoretical framework that reveals how learning and educational processes occur and evolve with the involvement of AI. Within this framework, as AI becomes involved, learning and teaching activities are no longer understood merely as individual human cognitive processes, but are increasingly conceptualized as a multilayered mechanism of knowledge construction that emerges through human–AI interaction. The core of this transformation is manifested in various forms such as externalization, internalization and hybrid intelligence. When learners delegate part of their cognitive activities to AI, this represents knowledge externalization. When learners engage with, reorganize and transfer the representations and feedback generated by AI, it reflects knowledge internalization. When humans and AI jointly extend problem representations and decision boundaries in a highly collaborative state, hybrid intelligence emerges.
Cukurova's conceptualization of human–AI interaction as different ways in which AI participates in cognition and learning aligns closely with theories of distributed cognition (Hutchins, [1995](#bjet70055-bib-0044)), which hold that cognitive activity does not reside solely within an individual's mind, but is distributed across interactive systems composed of individuals, social contexts and external artefacts. From this perspective, learning and thinking can be understood as processes jointly accomplished by multiple cognitive agents and the mediating tools they employ. Within this conceptual framing, AI is not understood merely as an auxiliary tool for cognition, but as a functional cognitive agent within the learning system that participates in information processing, problem representation and decision-making alongside humans. This interpretation helps explain how AI can act both as an extension of human cognition and as a resource that supports learners in reflecting on and reorganizing their learning activities, depending on how cognitive responsibility is distributed between humans and AI. Through this lens, educational and learning processes can be understood as increasingly collaborative and distributed, depending on how human–AI interaction is designed and enacted.
Therefore, applying this human–AI interaction perspective not only enables the description of interaction patterns but also provides a theoretically grounded lens for analysing how different forms of human–AI collaboration shape learning processes in empirical educational studies. It further lays the theoretical groundwork for rethinking the nature of learning and the distribution of cognition in AI-supported educational contexts.
### Literature reviews on GenAI in education
Previous systematic literature reviews have primarily focused on the latest developments, potential benefits and challenges of GenAI technologies, such as ChatGPT, in educational contexts (Ansari et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0004); Baidoo-Anu & Ansah, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0008); Bannister et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0009); Bhullar et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0012); Dempere et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0023); Guo, Zheng, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0039); Law, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0062)). These studies have demonstrated the potential of GenAI in providing personalized learning experiences, enhancing student engagement and addressing diverse learning needs. To illustrate, Lo ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0077)) and Ansari et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0004)) systematically reviewed the various tasks undertaken by GenAI in educational settings, its ways of application and corresponding evaluation methods, while identifying key challenges and suggesting future research directions.
Notably, Dempere et al. ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0023)) further reviewed existing research and demonstrated the impact of GenAI in higher education, such as improving student services and enhancing recruitment efficiency in support of teachers' responsibilities. Guo, Zheng, et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0039)) noted that current research predominantly focuses on higher education and STEM fields, including application scenarios like intelligent tutoring systems. While these studies offer a holistic perspective on the educational potential of GenAI, the attitudes and acceptance of educators and institutions are equally crucial for its large-scale implementation. A systematic review by Nikolic et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0092)) revealed that although most educators hold a positive attitude towards GenAI, realizing its full potential requires the development of comprehensive training programmes and the establishment of professional support teams to ensure the sustainable integration of GenAI into educational practices. To effectively advance the integration of GenAI into educational practice, rigorous empirical research and verifiable data are essential to assess the practical effectiveness and educational value of GenAI. Zhang et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)) conducted a systematic review of empirical studies that provide robust data to evaluate the effectiveness of GenAI in various educational settings, particularly offering insights into GenAI's contributions to diverse feedback methods and emerging assessment opportunities. These insights contribute to the scientific evaluation and effective implementation of GenAI in real-world educational contexts.
Although existing reviews have provided valuable insights into the application of GenAI in education, they have paid insufficient attention to the critical dimension of human–AI interaction. This dimension, which concerns how humans interact with and respond to AI, is one of the core factors influencing the effectiveness and outcomes of GenAI applications in educational settings and neglecting this key dimension may introduce significant uncertainty into research findings (Bach et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0007); Floridi et al., [2018](#bjet70055-bib-0031); Raees et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0104)). Existing studies often did not discuss the effectiveness or impact of GenAI from the perspective of human–AI interaction (Guo, Zheng, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0039); Nikolic et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0092); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)), thus failing to capture the dynamics specific to this perspective and limiting the generalizability of their findings to other educational scenarios that may involve different modes of human–AI interactions. Another critical issue that requires our attention is the lack of a holistic quantitative understanding of the effectiveness of GenAI in education. That is, the extent to which the empirical findings presented in existing studies, conducted in various scenarios and with different populations of educational stakeholders, can inform educational practice remains to be systematically quantified. Although numerous studies have reported the positive effects of GenAI tools (Deng et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0024)), their reliability and applicability across different educational contexts remain subject to ongoing scrutiny (Weidlich et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0125); Yan et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0130)). Therefore, there is an urgent need to adopt more rigorous evaluation methods to systematically assess the holistic robustness of these empirical conclusions and ensure their generalizability and practical value.
Therefore, conducting a systematic review and synthesis of research that applied GenAI in education and analysing these studies from a human–AI interaction perspective, along with detailed sensitivity analyses to assess the robustness of findings within each interaction mode, not only is essential for addressing current gaps in the literature but also forms a crucial foundation for promoting sustainable educational practices. In light of this, the present study aimed to conduct a systematic scoping review guided by the following research questions:
RQ1.What modes of human–AI interaction emerge across empirical studies on the use of GenAI in education, and how do these modes vary across educational contexts?
RQ2.Building on the identified human–AI interaction modes, to what extent do existing GenAI-based empirical findings provide adequate statistical power, thereby supporting the reliability of the report insights into each interaction mode?
## METHODS
### Review process
#### Search strategy
The systematic review followed the Preferred Reporting Items for Systematic Reviews and Meta-Analyses (PRISMA) protocol (Page et al., [2021](#bjet70055-bib-0096)) to ensure a transparent and systematic approach. To comprehensively capture relevant literature, we conducted searches in four major bibliographic databases: Web of Science, Scopus, ACM Digital Library and IEEE Xplore. These databases were chosen for their extensive coverage of peer-reviewed publications in the fields of computer science and education. Additionally, we used Google Scholar to identify newly published papers or those from venues not indexed in the selected databases. The search was conducted in December 2024, and the initial search strategy was iteratively refined through pilot searches to ensure inclusivity and relevance. The search query was designed to capture studies related to three main themes: (i) the use of mainstream GenAI technologies, (ii) the relevance to education and (iii) the collaborative focus placed between human and AI. Correspondingly, we defined one keyword group for each of the themes and these three keyword groups were concatenated using the AND operator, as shown in Table [1](#bjet70055-tbl-0001).
TABLE 1.
Keywords of query string.
| Themes | Keywords |
| --- | --- |
| GenAI technology | “generative artificial intelligence” OR “generative AI” OR “genAI” OR “\\*GPT\\*” OR “large language model” OR “large language models” OR “LLM” OR “LLMs” OR “generative language model” OR “generative language models” OR “generative model” OR “generative models” OR “pre-trained language model” OR “pre-trained language models” |
| Educational research | “education” OR “student\\*” OR “learner\\*” OR “teacher\\*” OR “instructor\\*” OR “educator\\*” |
| Focus on interaction between GenAI and humans (educators/students) | “collaborat\\*” OR “teaming” OR “cooperation” OR “coordination” OR “AI-assisted” OR “AI-empowered” OR “AI-facilitated” OR “human-AI hybrid” OR “AI-powered” OR “AI-Assistance” OR “AI-supported” OR “AI-based” |
\* \*Note\*: The keywords in different rows are connected by ‘AND’.
#### Paper selection
After obtaining the research articles, two researchers independently performed the title/abstract screening and full-text review. During the full-text review phase, a third researcher participated in the conflict resolution process to ensure the accurate selection of articles. We reviewed all included papers to ensure that these articles met the specific inclusion criteria outlined in Table [2](#bjet70055-tbl-0002).
TABLE 2.
Inclusion criteria.
| Inclusion criteria | Description |
| --- | --- |
| 1 | Publication involves the application of GenAI tools in lab or field settings and evaluates GenAI's effectiveness and impact on educational practice by collecting and analysing data from real-world teacher and student participants |
| 2 | Publication is a journal article or a full paper in prestigious conferences in relevant research fields. (eg, International Conference on Learning Analytics & Knowledge) |
| 3 | Publication is from 2010 to 2024 |
| 4 | Publication is written in English |
Initially, we retrieved a total of 3476 studies from the database searches. After removing 1097 duplicates, 2379 unique studies remained for screening. Two researchers independently reviewed the titles and abstracts of eligible articles based on four predefined exclusion criteria.
As a result, a total of 231 eligible studies proceeded to the full-text review stage. The inter-rater agreement score for this phase was 0.72, as measured by Cohen's kappa, indicating substantial agreement.
During the full-text review, we excluded a total of 181 studies. This exclusion criterion reflects the methodological orientation of our review, which required sufficient detail to systematically analyse human–AI interaction modes. In this process, we excluded studies that focused solely on qualitative analyses of participants' perceptions, as our review aimed to concentrate on empirical works that provided direct evidence of GenAI implementation and its educational impact. We also excluded studies that, although mentioning participant groups and experimental procedures, remained at a purely brief description without providing critical details of the specific human–AI interaction processes. In such cases, the lack of detailed experimental design rendered the studies insufficient to support our analysis and thus necessitated their exclusion.
This resulted in 50 studies being included for further analysis. The inter-rater agreement for the full-text review improved to 0.82, indicating a high level of consistency among the reviewers. Upon completing the entire review process, we conducted an additional search on Google Scholar, bringing the total number of studies included for subsequent analysis to 56.
In both screening stages, each article was reviewed by at least two researchers, with a third researcher resolving disagreements through in-depth discussions until consensus was reached. Additionally, to further ensure the reliability of the selection process, calibration exercises were conducted before the screening began, allowing the researchers to align their understanding of the inclusion criteria. Figure [2](#bjet70055-fig-0002) provides an overview of the entire review process, illustrating each stage from initial search to final inclusion.
[![](/cms/asset/a6b28388-8798-482b-9931-891a12b30980/bjet70055-fig-0002-m.png)](/cms/asset/13a6d8de-82a1-4c8a-83eb-c9b8bdc15a55/bjet70055-fig-0002-m.jpg)
\*\*FIGURE 2\*\*
[Open in figure viewer](#)[PowerPoint](/action/downloadFigures?id=bjet70055-fig-0002&partId=&doi=10.1111%2Fbjet.70055)
Review process based on the PRISMA protocol.
### Data coding and analysis
After completing the full-text review, we systematically extracted and coded the information in line with the research questions. Two researchers independently performed the initial coding and then compared their results. Following the social moderation coding approach (Shaffer & Ruis, [2021](#bjet70055-bib-0111)), differences were examined and resolved through discussion, and the coding framework was refined in several iterative rounds until a consistent interpretation was reached. This process established a clear and dependable coding scheme that supported rigorous data synthesis and analysis.
#### RQ1 coding: Interaction modes
To address RQ1, we adopt a structured analysis approach to identify the forms of interaction between GenAI and human participants within the educational context of a selected study. The analysis is guided by the established conceptual framework, namely the AIED-HCD conceptual framework (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)). This framework is based on two core dimensions: ‘AI Automation’ and ‘Human Control’, categorizing human–AI interaction into three primary forms, namely, ‘Internalization’, ‘Externalization’ and ‘Hybrid Intelligence’.
Although the AIED-HCD framework provides an overarching theoretical basis for classifying interaction modes into externalization, internalization and hybrid intelligence, existing empirical studies often did not explicitly use terms such as ‘Human Control’ or ‘AI Automation’ to describe interaction processes. Instead, these studies tended to describe interactions through the specific contexts in which they occurred and by identifying which party initiates the interaction (Tsiakas & Murray-Rust, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0119); Yang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0135)). To ensure coding consistency, we therefore used the initiator of the interaction, distinguishing whether the interaction was initiated by humans or initiated by AI, as an observable proxy when automation and control were under-specified. Using this indicator, we inferred the extent of AI automation and human control based on observable behaviours. For example, in a writing task, if a learner actively requested feedback from a GenAI tool after completing the task and the system only responded to such requests, the human was considered the initiator. This situation reflected a high human-controlled interaction pattern, as the learner led the learning process while the AI provided support only when called upon. This generally corresponds to the internalization mode (Niloy et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0093); Tsai et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0118)). In contrast, if the GenAI tool automatically generated feedback without being prompted and the learner could only receive it passively, the AI was considered the initiator. This situation represents a high AI-automated interaction pattern, where the task is mainly executed by the AI and the human takes on a more receptive role, which aligns more closely with the externalization mode (Guo, Pan, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0037); Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076)). Initiation alone does not fully determine the balance of control and automation. Instead, it functioned as a triangulation cue together with other forms of evidence such as explicit descriptions of human oversight or references to the degree of AI automation. In this way, we ensured both theoretical consistency and methodological rigour when mapping empirical descriptions onto the AIED-HCD framework (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)).
#### RQ1 coding: Pedagogy embedding
To complement the analysis of interaction modes, we further examined the educational contextual factors of these empirical studies to uncover broader trends and educational values in the use of GenAI for teaching and learning. Specifically, we analysed the educational goals for which GenAI was employed, the degree of GenAI involvement in educational practices, the disciplinary domain, the educational level, the educational tasks supported by GenAI, the stakeholder groups involved and whether the studies reported specific learning or teaching outcomes.
First, to reveal the educational intentions underlying the use of GenAI in teaching and learning practices, we coded the educational objectives of GenAI use following prior studies (Flavin & Quintero, [2018](#bjet70055-bib-0030); Kirkwood & Price, [2014](#bjet70055-bib-0053); Palme, [2009](#bjet70055-bib-0097)). When GenAI was primarily used to reduce the time and effort required of teachers or students in routine educational tasks and to optimize existing instructional processes without fundamentally changing teaching practices, the study was coded as \*efficiency\*. When GenAI was employed to improve the quality of teaching and learning within the existing instructional framework by enhancing the precision and relevance of learning processes such as providing instant feedback to increase learner engagement, offering adaptive prompts to improve learning outcomes or assisting teachers in producing more diagnostic and constructive feedback, the study was coded as \*enhancement\*. When GenAI contributed to a deeper pedagogical change by reshaping the organizational logic or role relationships within educational activities such as enabling new instructional models like human and AI co-teaching and AI tutoring systems that promote personalized, generative and metacognitive learning, the study was coded as \*transformation\*. When a study presented multiple objectives, we identified the dominant educational goal according to its primary purpose, design focus and evaluation indicators and assigned a single code.
Second, to reveal differences in the depth and scope of GenAI integration across studies, we drew on the hierarchical classifications that are commonly used in adaptive learning and human–AI interaction research to distinguish the degree of GenAI involvement. Following previous studies (Memon & Kwan, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0082); Pelánek, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0098); Peng et al., [2019](#bjet70055-bib-0099)), we established several levels of analytical granularity. A study was coded as \*within-task\* when GenAI provided support only during a specific stage or component of a single teaching or learning task. For example, GenAI could offer feedback to students during the revision phase of a writing task or provide language editing suggestions to teachers during grading. In these cases, the remaining parts of the task were completed independently by humans. A study was coded as \*task level\* when GenAI supported the execution of an entire teaching or learning task and was available or designed to be used across multiple phases of that task. For example, GenAI might assist students throughout the whole writing process from planning and drafting to revision. It might also support teachers during lesson design, instruction and feedback. A study was coded as \*course level\* when the application of GenAI extended beyond a single teaching or learning task and supported a broader instructional or learning process that included multiple related tasks. For example, GenAI helped teachers or learners achieve higher level instructional and learning goals across a course or instructional unit. For example, it might provide guidance on learning paths, integrated performance feedback or course-level summaries.
Third, to ensure consistency, rigour and comparability with existing literature, the classifications for the disciplinary domain, educational level, educational tasks supported by GenAI and stakeholder groups involved were adapted from prior systematic reviews of empirical research in educational technology (Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)). Specifically, we first coded the educational disciplines involved in each study, classifying them according to the fields reported in the studies (eg, Computer Science, Engineering). We then coded the educational levels based on the participants populations described (eg, higher education, K–12), in order to identify differences in the use of GenAI across educational contexts. Third, the stakeholder groups were coded into two categories, educators and students, to identify the primary target beneficiaries addressed in current research efforts. Finally, we coded the specific educational tasks that are supported by GenAI in teaching and learning. In this study, we followed the definition of educational tasks proposed in previous research (Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)) and defined them as discrete instructional or learning activity units that can be supported or enhanced through GenAI. To ensure accurate identification and classification, we drew on the task list summarized in earlier studies (Yan, Sha, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0133); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)) and made appropriate adjustments to fit the context and data characteristics of this study. During the classification process, we applied clear operational rules. A study was placed in a task category when the function of GenAI described in the paper matched the definition of that task. If a study covered functions that belonged to more than one category, we used a multilabel approach that allowed one paper to be assigned to multiple categories. This framing enabled us to capture the concrete ways in which GenAI is applied in authentic teaching and learning contexts while preserving comparability across studies. The detailed descriptions of these tasks are provided in Table [3](#bjet70055-tbl-0003).
TABLE 3.
Educational tasks supported by GenAI: Characterizing how GenAI contributes to teaching and learning, with descriptions (Yan, Sha, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0133); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)).
| Educational task | Stakeholder | Description |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Curriculum content creation | Educator | GenAI is applied to developing textual resources, interactive components (eg, quizzes) and multimedia content such as images, audio, video and simulations |
| Student | GenAI is applied to assisting students in producing study notes, review outlines and knowledge maps and in co-designing classroom activities |
| Written assessment assistance | Educator | GenAI is applied to supporting grading by producing scores or objective evaluations, without providing formative feedback |
| Student | GenAI is applied to directly participating in the production of content to support students in assessment tasks, helping them complete and enhance their performance by generating content that can be integrated into their work |
| Educational feedback and dialogue | Educator | GenAI is applied to supporting feedback generation, producing formative comments, improvement suggestions and error summaries |
| Student | GenAI is applied to offering feedback such as personalized suggestions and optimization advice, without directly rewriting the content or participating in its production, and the feedback is delivered through dialogue or one-way comments |
| Question answering | Educator | GenAI is applied to providing explanations, supplementary knowledge, examples and pedagogical suggestions during lesson preparation or classroom teaching. This category focuses on on-demand informational support, not resource creation or feedback generation |
| Student | GenAI is applied to providing targeted explanations in response to students' general knowledge or inquiry questions as an on-demand cognitive facilitator |
Finally, we introduced the dimension of reported outcome to systematically present the effects of applying GenAI in teaching and learning as reported in current empirical studies. This dimension was coded based on the results reported in each study. When a study indicated that the use of GenAI in the teaching or learning process led to a significant improvement in learning outcomes, it was coded as an \*increase\*. A study was coded as a \*decrease\* when the results showed a significant decline in outcomes or impact. A study was coded as \*no significant difference\* when no statistically significant difference was found before and after the application of GenAI, or when only an increasing or decreasing trend was observed without reaching a significant level. A study was coded as \*mixed\* when it reported different results across multiple evaluation measures, meaning that some measures showed significant positive or negative changes while others showed no significant difference. For example, when a study reported a significant improvement in academic performance but no significant change in self-efficacy or engagement. A study was coded as \*not reported\* when it did not report any results related to learning outcomes. Considering that indicators like ‘Perceptions’, such as perceived usefulness and perceived ease of use, reflect users' subjective views of the tool rather than its objective educational impact, we excluded them from the coding process to maintain objectivity.
#### RQ2: Sensitivity analysis
To address Research Question 2, we systematically analysed the sensitivity and robustness of statistical findings reported in GenAI-based empirical research. Specifically, we extracted key statistical details including test type, sample size, significance level, reported effect size, parametric or non-parametric status and the fulfilment of relevant statistical assumptions. Where effect sizes were not directly reported or used alternative formats, we manually calculated standardized effect sizes based on established guidelines (eg, Cohen's \*d\* for \*t\*-tests, Cohen's \*f\* for ANOVAs and Cohen's \*w\* for chi-square analyses) (Cohen, [2013](#bjet70055-bib-0016); Lakens, [2013](#bjet70055-bib-0060)). Using the G\\*Power tool (Version 3.1) (Faul et al., [2007](#bjet70055-bib-0029); Faul et al., [2009](#bjet70055-bib-0028)), we performed a sensitivity analysis to identify the minimum detectable effect size required to achieve the widely accepted target statistical power of 0.80, thus ensuring balanced interpretation of Type I and Type II errors (Faul et al., [2007](#bjet70055-bib-0029); Lakens, [2022](#bjet70055-bib-0061)). This approach aligns with methodological recommendations and best practices from statistical theory, strengthening the validity and credibility of our findings (Cohen, [2013](#bjet70055-bib-0016)). Finally, based on the calculated results, we coded whether the effect size in each study met the minimum requirement, categorizing them as \*Yes\*, \*Partially Satisfied\* or \*No\*. Note that a single study may contain multiple statistical tests. If all tests meet the minimum effect size, the study is labelled as \*Yes\*. If the study includes both tests that meet and do not meet the minimum effect size, it is labelled as \*Partially Satisfied\*. If none of the tests meet the minimum effect size, the study is labelled as \*No\*.
## RESULTS
### Results on RQ1
According to the definition of human–AI interaction modes in the AIED-HCD conceptual framework (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)), we analysed the 56 empirical studies reviewed to examine their distribution across different interaction modes. This section begins by outlining the objectives guiding the use of GenAI in the reviewed studies, followed by an overarching overview of emerging trends in GenAI-based empirical research across diverse educational contexts.
#### Externalization
Based on the results shown in Tables [4](#bjet70055-tbl-0004) and [5](#bjet70055-tbl-0005), among the 56 reviewed empirical studies, 13 adopted interaction patterns consistent with the externalization mode, characterized by low human control and high AI automation. Research under this mode primarily focused on the goal of Enhancement, rather than Efficiency or Transformation. Such studies typically aimed to improve the quality and precision of existing teaching and learning processes, rather than to reconstruct pedagogical paradigms or redefine teacher–student role relationships. In this mode, GenAI was usually embedded within specific components of a single task (\*N\* = 8), such as providing feedback during the review phase (Guo, Pan, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0037)). In other words, by assigning clearly defined and structured instructional tasks to GenAI, researchers were able to maintain a high level of automation while avoiding disruptions to existing instructional workflows.

\* \*Note\*: Disciplines were coded based on the study's primary educational domain (eg, computer science, teacher education, physical education).
Subsequently, these studies were further categorized according to educational level and stakeholder role. The results indicated that the externalization mode predominantly appeared in higher education contexts (\*N\* = 9) with only a few studies applied in K–12 settings (\*N\* = 4) and that most studies focused on supporting students (\*N\* = 10) rather than educators (\*N\* = 3). This distribution suggests that the externalization mode was primarily employed for student-oriented learning support tasks, especially in text-generation and feedback-intensive disciplines, where most studies (\*N\* = 11) use GenAI as an automated feedback assistant to compensate for instructors' limited capacity to provide feedback in teaching environments.
In the provision of educational feedback and dialogue support, GenAI has been employed to automatically perform feedback tasks that were traditionally undertaken by educators. For example, in writing tasks, GenAI automatically analyses student submissions and generates personalized feedback, which is delivered directly to students without educator intervention (Boudouaia et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0013); Guo, Pan, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0037); Meyer et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0084); Neyem et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0089); Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)). In some studies (Boudouaia et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0013); Meyer et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0084); Neyem et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0089)), feedback was generated after submission, with the system independently processing the text and providing suggestions for each question. The entire process was system-driven, with no control from students, reflecting low human control and high AI automation. Although students were allowed to revise their writing based on the feedback, the generation of the feedback itself was entirely driven by the system. Students had no influence over the timing or content of the feedback, which underscores their passive role in the feedback process. In the context of the programming task (Ouyang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0095)), GenAI also assumed the feedback role. After group tasks, the system generated feedback across multiple dimensions, such as correctness and readability, without involvement from educators or students. This highlights a highly automated feedback process where both educator and learner control are minimal.
Beyond feedback generation, GenAI has also been used to automate more advanced instructional tasks. In one study (Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076)), GenAI provided automated strategic and linguistic support throughout multiple stages of the writing process, including preparation, presentation, practice, evaluation and extension. These instructional supports replaced teacher involvement. In contrast to the control group, where instruction was delivered manually by teachers, the experimental group received system-initiated guidance that was predefined and automatically executed. This illustrates a greater degree of automation in the instructional process.
It is worth noting that studies adopting the externalization mode have been predominantly conducted in disciplines such as language education (\*N\* = 6) (Boudouaia et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0013); Guo, Pan, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0037); Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076); Meyer et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0084); Sayin & Gierl, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0108); Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)), computer science (\*N\* = 4) (Naik et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0088); Neyem et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0089); Ouyang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0095); Singh et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0112)), chemistry (\*N\* = 1) (Li, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0067)), physics (\*N\* = 1) (Kortemeyer, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0056)) and medical (\*N\* = 1) (Hudon et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0043)). These disciplines typically involve learning outcomes such as code unit test results (Messer et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0083)) or chemical reaction equations (Eitemüller et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0026)), which are governed by clear, objective and quantifiable evaluation criteria. These characteristics make such disciplines particularly suitable for stable and reliable automation. This allows routine feedback tasks to be delegated to GenAI, with the goal of enhancing instructional effectiveness while managing associated teaching risks. The high level of automation in this mode demonstrates clear advantages in teaching contexts involving large student cohorts or frequent feedback demands. Through automation mechanisms, delegating certain learning tasks to GenAI effectively alleviates instructors' workload, while providing students with increased opportunities to receive support (Hudon et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0043)).
However, although this form of task outsourcing mode generally demonstrates positive effects (\*N\* = 9), its educational value lies primarily in enhancing feedback availability and improving instructional processes rather than in deeply fostering conceptual understanding or knowledge internalization, a finding that resonates with Cukurova's theoretical analysis of the potential educational limitations of the externalization mode (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)). The feedback generated by GenAI may help students identify problems and make revisions, but its impact on students' comprehension and absorption of underlying knowledge remains limited, due to the potentially limited ability of learners to adopt and internalize the feedback (O'Donovan et al., [2021](#bjet70055-bib-0094)). In other words, the externalization mode provides a form of human support enhanced by automation at the instructional level, but this support mainly reflects an improvement in teaching processes rather than in learners' cognitive development. Thus, while the externalization mode has shown certain positive effects on instructional organization and learning facilitation, its role in supporting students' deep understanding and knowledge acquisition appears to be limited.
#### Internalization
As shown in Tables [4](#bjet70055-tbl-0004) and [6](#bjet70055-tbl-0006), the internalization mode has become the most common form of human–AI interaction in current empirical studies. Its research focus generally follows a pattern similar to that of the externalization mode. In terms of educational goals, most studies (\*N\* = 29) focus on using GenAI to enhance existing teaching and learning practices with the aim of improving instructional quality and learning outcomes. A smaller number of studies explore the potential of GenAI for educational transformation (\*N\* = 5). This distribution clearly shows that researchers are more concerned with deepening the quality of teaching and learning.
TABLE 6.

| Disciplines in education | Education task |
| --- | --- |
| Curriculum content creation | Written assessment assistance | Educational feedback and dialogue | Question answering |

Although the general trend of educational goals resembles that of the externalization mode, the internalization mode shows a distinct difference in the level of GenAI involvement. Most studies focus on the task level (\*N\* = 19), indicating a preference for supporting the entire process of a complete learning or teaching task, such as the full writing workflow from planning to revision. This continuous support allows GenAI to accompany learners throughout the task and provide ongoing cognitive assistance. In addition, a considerable number of studies (\*N\* = 9) extend to the course level, offering support across multiple related tasks and integrating GenAI into broader instructional processes such as guiding learning pathways or providing holistic performance feedback. In contrast, applications within a single subtask (\*N\* = 6) are relatively limited. This pattern suggests that researchers tend to examine the role of GenAI in more comprehensive and authentic learning settings. Such a preference aligns closely with the core educational aims of enhancement and transformation. To truly achieve meaningful improvement in learning quality, GenAI needs to provide continuous and holistic support throughout the learning process. Likewise, higher level educational transformation, such as the development of metacognitive or reflective learning abilities, can only be realized through sustained engagement within a complete learning cycle rather than through fragmented intervention.
The internalization mode also emphasizes learners' critical absorption, reflection and integration of the information provided by GenAI. This requires a higher level of cognitive maturity and self-directed learning ability from participants. Consequently, most studies focus on the higher education context (\*N\* = 26), where students are the primary stakeholders. In contrast, studies in K–12 education are relatively few (\*N\* = 8), reflecting the challenges of applying such cognitively demanding interaction to younger learners whose mental development is still in progress. Similarly, studies centred on educators remain rare (\*N\* = 2). This indicates that there is still substantial potential for future research to explore how the internalization mode can be used to support teachers' professional growth and enhance their reflective instructional practices.
Unlike the Externalization mode, where tasks originally performed by teachers or students are modelled and delegated to GenAI for automatic execution, the Internalization mode is characterized by human-led learning and teaching processes. In this mode, learners or educators interact with GenAI to obtain insights that facilitate reflection and improvement, thereby prompting adjustments in their learning or teaching strategies. From the perspective of human control and AI automation, the two types of systems differ significantly. Internalization mode typically allows learners to retain full control over when and how to invoke GenAI.
As shown in Table [6](#bjet70055-tbl-0006), GenAI in this mode primarily provides instructional support through question answering functions (\*N\* = 19). For instance, in a library information literacy project, students only posed questions to GenAI when they had a clear need. The system's role was limited to retrieving and generating responses, while decisions about what to ask and whether to adopt the answers were entirely up to the students (Hu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0041)). A similar mechanism was applied in another study (Chen & Chang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0015)), where the interface included a question button that students had to click manually or activate after reviewing example questions. GenAI would then provide relevant suggestions but would not intervene in the task flow on its own. Another classroom experiment further demonstrated this high level of human control and low AI automation. In this case (Kavadella et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0049)), students in the GenAI group formulated prompts, interacted with the model and compared its outputs with reference materials before deciding whether to accept them. Such designs encourage learners to engage in ongoing reflection and evaluation throughout the process (Wang et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0123)).
Beyond question answering, GenAI is also widely used for written assessment assistance (\*N\* = 11) and educational feedback and dialogue support (\*N\* = 10). In these cases, GenAI may exhibit characteristics typically associated with the externalization mode, as it can automatically generate feedback with minimal human involvement. As demonstrated in a previous study (Ouyang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0095)), GenAI automatically produced multidimensional feedback after task completion, without any involvement from teachers or students and students acted merely as recipients of the results. In contrast, the internalization mode emphasizes that users should critically engage with GenAI insights to adapt their learning. When students use GenAI to assist with programming (Pham et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0101)), they often engage in an iterative process of rephrasing questions, adding contextual information and challenging the model's outputs before making informed decisions about whether to adopt its suggestions (Liu, Li, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0072)). Another experiment further illustrates this approach (Yilmaz & Yilmaz, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0136)), in which students leveraged GenAI to assist in constructing a programme. Since the GenAI system could only process text and not interpret UML diagrams, students needed to carefully deconstruct the task, pose precise questions and iteratively refine the generated content. These interactions demonstrate the system's low level of automation and high degree of learner control, while also fostering deeper task understanding through continuous reflection.
In the domain of curriculum content creation (\*N\* = 10), students often treat GenAI as a writing revision tool, combining its suggestions with their own thinking to refine their work (Liu, Li, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0072); Tsai et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0118)). In programming tasks, GenAI serves as an assistant that supports code generation and debugging (Kazemitabaar, Hou, et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0052); Yilmaz & Yilmaz, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0136)). For content creation, GenAI typically generates preliminary materials based on user input, which are then reviewed and edited by humans before implementation (Liu et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0073)). In concept development, GenAI can also provide initial drafts or key ideas upon request, thereby supporting knowledge construction (Chandrasekera et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0014)).
In this interaction mode, the role of GenAI is to offer suggestions, content or visualized insight that assist human users in reflection and improvement. Final interpretation and decision-making authority always remain with the human. This mode has been applied in 13 disciplines including language education, computer science, business and design. Its widespread adoption across various academic fields demonstrates the high adaptability and transferability of low-automation systems, which position AI as a facilitator of human reflection, self-regulation and collaborative optimization rather than a replacement for human instructors.
Regarding the reported outcomes, most studies presented clear positive effects (\*N\* = 18), indicating that under conditions of high human control and reflective engagement, the involvement of GenAI can effectively enhance students' learning performance and process quality. A few studies reported no significant differences (\*N\* = 4), which were often associated with short usage duration or a lack of guided interaction (Kazemitabaar, Hou, et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0052); Sun et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0115)), suggesting that the quality of human–AI interaction remains a key factor influencing effectiveness. At the same time, GenAI assists teachers in self-reflection and pedagogical improvement. For example, studies have shown that collaboration with ChatGPT promotes pre-service teachers' professional growth and the optimization of their instructional strategies (Li et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0069)). Such collaboration not only helps teachers better understand their own strengths and weaknesses in teaching but also provides instant feedback and multifaceted insights that stimulate deeper reflection on instructional content, methods and learners' needs, thereby fostering continuous professional development and pedagogical innovation.
However, compared with the externalization mode, the internalization mode shows a more complex trend in learning outcomes. This pattern is consistent with existing research on human–AI interaction (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)), particularly work that conceptualizes internalization as relying on learners' active engagement, reflective depth and strategic regulation, with AI primarily functioning as a cognitive scaffold rather than a source of direct output. Viewed from this perspective, the present review suggests that the short-term effects of internalization-oriented GenAI use may be less immediately observable than those associated with externalization. Nevertheless, synthesizing empirical findings across studies indicates that this mode demonstrates more enduring potential for long-term learning transfer, the development of meta-cognitive abilities and the enhancement of teachers' reflective instructional practices.
#### Hybrid intelligence
Based on the findings presented in Tables [4](#bjet70055-tbl-0004) and [7](#bjet70055-tbl-0007), a total of nine studies have been classified under the hybrid intelligence mode. These studies collectively demonstrate a shift in educational objectives from enhancement to transformation. In the dimension of Educational Objectives of GenAI Use, the proportion of studies reflecting a transformation-oriented approach is higher than in other modes of human–AI interaction. These studies focus on how GenAI can reshape instructional structures, learning roles and cognitive processes, indicating that the emphasis of research has moved from technology-enabled improvement towards human–AI co-creation in educational innovation.
TABLE 7.
Distribution of disciplines in studies using the Hybrid Intelligence Mode, grouped by education task.
| Disciplines in education | Education task |
| --- | --- |
| Curriculum content creation | Written assessment assistance | Educational feedback and dialogue | Question answering |
| Language | Ghafouri et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034)); Peng et al. ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)) | Ghafouri et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034)); Peng et al. ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)); Song and Song ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)) | Ghafouri et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034)); Peng et al. ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)); Song and Song ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)) | Ghafouri et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034)); Song and Song ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)) |
