| Teacher | Markel et al. ([2023](#bjet70055-bib-0080)) |
| Health Sciences |
| Social Science |
| Mathematics |
| Law |
\* \*Note\*: Disciplines were coded based on the study's primary educational domain (eg, computer science, teacher education, physical education).
From the perspective of Degree of GenAI Involvement, GenAI in the hybrid intelligence mode tends to play a deeper role, mainly at the task level and the course level. At the task level, GenAI provides continuous support throughout different stages of instructional activities. At the course level, it demonstrates integrative capabilities such as generating personalized learning path recommendations, comprehensive performance feedback and learning summaries (Darban, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0020); Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034); Lee, Chen, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0064); Peng et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)). This higher level of involvement shows that GenAI in the hybrid intelligence mode has evolved from an assisting tool to a collaborative partner that shares teaching responsibilities with humans. It has become a key driving force that supports generative learning and co-constructive knowledge building, marking a transition of educational goals from localized optimization towards deeper pedagogical transformation.
All of these studies were conducted in higher education contexts. Although students were the main beneficiaries, two of the studies also involved the collaboration of educators. As shown in Table [7](#bjet70055-tbl-0007), these studies primarily explored how GenAI can support human participants in tasks related to educational feedback and dialogue as well as question answering. Compared with the externalization and internalization modes, the hybrid intelligence mode exhibits a higher degree of human–AI interaction. GenAI not only generates outcomes or suggestions but also participates in meaning-making and decision processes. In other words, humans and GenAI form a complementary cognitive system during learning activities, jointly fostering knowledge generation and reflection. This close interactive mechanism represents the key feature that distinguishes the hybrid intelligence mode from the other two modes.
Specifically, one study in a writing task allowed students to interact with GenAI in real time (Song & Song, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)). The system automatically performed tasks such as grammar correction, vocabulary suggestions, sentence structure optimization and writing progress tracking, demonstrating a high level of AI automation. At the same time, students retained substantive control over the writing process. They could decide when to invoke the AI, whether to adopt its suggestions. They were also able to raise clarification questions at any time. This system design supported learners' self-reflection and iterative revision, further reinforcing the dominant role of the human. Another study (Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034)) illustrated how teachers collaborated with GenAI in lesson planning and assessment. The system could automatically generate lesson plans, example materials and formative assessment based on the teaching topic, students' language proficiency and course objectives. Although the planning and feedback processes were highly automated, teachers were still responsible for selecting and reorganizing the AI-generated content, and for adjusting their instructional strategies according to real-time classroom needs. This dynamic division of labour allowed teachers' professional judgement to be fully exercised. These systems combine a high degree of automation with active human oversight, exemplifying the key characteristics of hybrid intelligence (Wu et al., [n.d.](#bjet70055-bib-0128); Darban, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0020); Lin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0071); Markel et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0080); Peng et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)).
In contrast, the externalization mode is represented by fully automated feedback systems (Neyem et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0089)). In this mode, after students submitted their work, GenAI independently analysed the text and generated feedback, which was then delivered directly to the students. Learners had no control over the timing of the feedback or the underlying processing. These systems were designed to emphasize full task automation, with students playing a primarily passive role as recipients of information. In studies adopting the Internalization mode (Chen & Chang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0015); Kavadella et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0049)), students were required to formulate prompts in advance based on their learning goals before interacting with GenAI. After the interaction, they reviewed the generated content and compared it with course materials to decide whether to adopt it.
GenAI only returned information when actively invoked by the learner and did not intervene in the task process on its own. These systems had a lower level of AI automation but allowed learners greater flexibility in adjusting their strategies. By comparing these three modes, it becomes evident that hybrid intelligence integrates automation into various stages of the learning process through real-time interaction, while still ensuring that humans maintain control over both content and process. Externalization mode delegates almost the entire task to GenAI, whereas internalization mode confines GenAI to the role of an information provider and emphasizes learner autonomy and strategic development. Hybrid intelligence establishes a nuanced middle ground between the two, leveraging the strengths of both modes to enable collaboration. These differences reveal the fundamental distinctions among the three modes in terms of human–AI interaction and the distribution of control and responsibility.
It is worth noting that, in the dimension of Reported Outcome, most studies adopting the hybrid intelligence mode have demonstrated improvements in learning performance, including aspects such as reflective ability (Lin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0071)) and self-regulation (Lee, Chen, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0064)). However, several studies have presented inconsistent results. Some indicators such as patient communication and clinical judgement showed improvement, while others such as theoretical knowledge tests showed no significant changes (Ba et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0006)) or even a decline in performance (Peng et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)). These differences suggest that, although the hybrid intelligence mode generally shows a positive trend, the underlying mechanisms of its effectiveness remain complex and not yet fully understood. Further empirical research is needed to confirm and strengthen these promising effects.
Although the nine studies of hybrid intelligence span multiple disciplines, reflecting the fragmented nature typical of early exploratory research, existing findings indicate that hybrid intelligence represents a promising yet technically demanding educational paradigm. At present, it remains in its initial stage of development. By definition, hybrid intelligence aims to achieve high levels of artificial intelligence automation while maintaining strong human control and enabling real-time collaborative interaction. Its goal is to transform artificial intelligence from a passive tool into an active co-creator. The paradigm is considered technically demanding because it requires advanced AI capabilities that can support dynamic, real-time and deeply integrated collaboration with humans.
As GenAI continues to evolve rapidly, there remains room for improvement compared with the high-level collaborative state envisioned by hybrid intelligence (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)). This developmental stage partly explains the complexity and diversity observed in existing research outcomes, where positive trends coexist with varying and sometimes inconsistent results. Present technologies still face challenges in maintaining tightly coupled and co-constructive interaction with humans, as they struggle to sustain contextual understanding and adaptive responsiveness throughout communication processes (Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034)). Their generated feedback also often lacks consistency and stability (Song & Song, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)). These limitations in interactive capability suggest that current studies should be regarded as preliminary explorations towards the realization of the hybrid intelligence paradigm. Overall, these findings suggest that current implementations of hybrid intelligence remain constrained primarily by technological limitations rather than by conceptual shortcomings.
Achieving hybrid intelligence would require co-constructing meaning through interaction and continuously adapting to enhance the capacities of both humans and AI. This stands in sharp contrast to the externalization mode, which focuses on automating predefined tasks, and the internalization mode, which provides insights without engaging in real-time co-construction.
#### Chi-square analysis for interaction mode
To further explore the potential connections and structural features between dimensions of the educational context and modes of human–AI interaction, we conducted chi-square tests. The results in Table [8](#bjet70055-tbl-0008) revealed differentiated associations between variables and interaction modes. In terms of the objectives of GenAI use, the distribution of the three interaction modes across efficiency, enhancement and transformation was statistically significant (\*p\* < 0.05\\*), indicating that GenAI use objectives differed substantially by interaction type. Specifically, both externalization and internalization modes were predominantly associated with enhancement-oriented objectives, suggesting that current applications of GenAI mainly focus on supporting or extending existing teaching and learning processes rather than replacing them. In contrast, the hybrid intelligence mode showed a relatively higher association with transformation objectives, reflecting emerging efforts to leverage human–AI interaction for deeper pedagogical innovation and knowledge co-construction.
TABLE 8.
Summary of association tests between human–AI interaction modes and educational contextual factors.
| Educational contextual factors | \*p\*-value | Cramer's V |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Educational objectives of GenAI use | <0.05\\* | 0.370 |
| Degree of GenAI Involvement | <0.05\\* | 0.396 |
| Educational task | <0.05\\* | 0.312 |
| Educational level | <0.05\\* | 0.568 |
| Stakeholder role | 0.196 | 0.239 |
| Reported outcome | 0.242 | 0.274 |
\* \*Note\*: An asterisk (\\*) is used to indicate statistically significant results, where \*p\* < 0.05.
With respect to the dimension of GenAI involvement, the distribution of interaction modes shows a significant difference (\*p\* < 0.05\\*), indicating meaningful variation in how GenAI is integrated and positioned. The internalization mode appears more frequently across levels, suggesting that many studies design GenAI use around its language generation and reasoning capabilities to support teachers or learners during task execution, learning diagnosis and feedback generation, thereby fostering knowledge internalization and meaning making. In other words, these designs place the promotion of human understanding and reflection at the centre and treat GenAI as a cognitive partner that supports deep learning rather than a purely automated tool. By contrast, externalization and hybrid intelligence modes tend to concentrate at specific levels. Externalization appears more often at the task level, and hybrid intelligence is more often used at the course level in complex scenarios such as instructional design and adaptive interventions. This layered distribution reveals a design logic and division of functions in educational processes. At the task level, researchers prefer to let AI handle content generation or execution in an externalizing role. At higher teaching and curricular levels, they explore mixed modes of co-creation and decision support. Overall, this pattern may be interpreted as indicating a gradual shift in the educational integration of GenAI from a tool-oriented stance towards a more partner-oriented role, with interaction design moving beyond output efficiency towards supporting cognitive construction and meaning-making in learning.
In addition, for the dimension of educational task type, we also observed a significant association with interaction modes (\*p\* < 0.05\\*). This result indicates differences in how task types distribute across interaction modes. Externalization is more likely in procedural and standardizable workflows that prioritize automatic generation and fast responses. Internalization is more common in contexts centred on understanding and reflection, for example, educational feedback and dialogue, where language generation and reasoning promote meaning-making and self-regulation. hybrid intelligence appears more often in open-ended and orchestrated tasks that require co-creation and coordination, for example, curriculum content creation and multi-turn dialogue with feedback, reflecting human and AI complementarity in problem representation, solution generation and decision processes.
Meanwhile, a significant difference between educational level and interaction modes (\*p\* < 0.05\\*) suggests structural variation in AI application scenarios and interaction modes across educational tiers. In higher education, hybrid intelligence and internalization appear more frequently, whereas in K–12 settings, externalization is more common. This divergence may arise from differences in instructional goals and learner characteristics. Higher education emphasizes critical thinking and independent inquiry, and instructors tend to use GenAI as a partner for joint analysis, reflection and knowledge generation. K–12 education emphasizes knowledge acquisition and skills training, and AI is often used as a supportive tool to reduce teacher workload or to provide immediate feedback to students. Institutional and ethical constraints may also contribute to this difference. For example, stronger requirements for data privacy and model interpretability in K–12 contexts can limit deep forms of interaction. Overall, the findings point to a hierarchical evolution in the depth of integration and role positioning of GenAI. In basic education, it functions primarily as a cognitive assistant, while in higher education, it evolves towards an intelligent co-creation partner.
From a practical perspective, these three significant results together reveal structural differentiation of human-AI interaction within the education system. As instructional level and educational tier rise, the depth of participation by GenAI, the complexity of interaction and the intensity of cognitive engagement all tend to increase. These findings provide empirical support for understanding how generative AI is embedded in teaching and also offer guidance for future layered frameworks for AI integration in education. At different tiers and stages, interaction modes should be matched to task complexity and learning goals in order to build a more efficient and more human-centred educational intelligence ecosystem. From a methodological perspective, because some expected cell counts were small, the chi-square approximation may be less precise. Even so, the overall significance patterns and moderate-to-large effect sizes (Cramer's V ≈ 0.3–0.6) indicate robust and practically meaningful associations. Accordingly, these results should be interpreted as statistically indicative yet substantively significant trends, providing credible evidence for the structural differentiation of human–AI interaction across educational contexts.
### Results on RQ2
The selected statistical tests are summarized in Table [9](#bjet70055-tbl-0009). The results of the assumption tests for parametric statistics are presented in Table [10](#bjet70055-tbl-0010), and the minimum effect sizes required based on the sample size are shown in Table [11](#bjet70055-tbl-0011).

#### Externalization
For the externalization mode (ie, low human control and high AI automation), most of the selected statistical tests were parametric, appearing in eight studies, whereas non-parametric tests were used in only three studies. Among the parametric tests, independent samples \*t\*-tests (Boudouaia et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0013); Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076); Ouyang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0095)) were commonly employed, while correlation analysis appeared only once (Kortemeyer, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0056)). More advanced parametric models, such as generalized linear mixed effects models (Singh et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0112)), linear mixed effects models (Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076); Singh et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0112)) and cumulative link mixed models (Singh et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0112)), were also utilized. Regression analysis (Meyer et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0084)), ANOVA (Naik et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0088)) and ANCOVA (Li, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0067)) were also used to examine variance and outcomes. Several of these studies applied multiple parametric tests, for example, (Boudouaia et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0013)) used both independent and paired samples \*t\*-tests, and (Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076)) used three different statistical tests (generalized linear mixed effects, linear mixed effects and cumulative link mixed models). Among the eight studies that employed parametric statistical tests, only two explicitly reported conducting relevant assumption tests to verify that the conditions required for the use of parametric methods were met. In contrast, the remaining five studies did not clearly indicate whether such assumption tests were performed. This lack of transparency raises concerns about the validity of the statistical conclusions in those studies, as parametric tests rely on specific assumptions (eg, normality, homogeneity of variances and independence), if violated, can compromise the integrity of the results. For non-parametric methods, chi-square tests (Hudon et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0043)), Mann–Whitney \*U\*-tests (Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)) and Wilcoxon Signed-Rank Tests (Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)) were employed, especially in cases where assumptions of normality were not met. Friedman tests (Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)) and Quade's test (Guo, Pan, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0037)) were also used to support analyses of ranked or ordinal data across related samples.
Regarding sample sizes, three studies (Kortemeyer, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0056); Naik et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0088); Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)) had samples of 50 or fewer participants. Of these, one study (Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)) partially met the minimum effect size threshold, while another (Naik et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0088)) did not meet it at all. Only one study fully met the minimum effect size criterion (Kortemeyer, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0056)). The most common sample size range was between 51 and 100 participants, represented by four studies (Boudouaia et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0013); Li, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0067); Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076); Ouyang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0095)). Among these, two studies (Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076); Ouyang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0095)) fully met the minimum effect size requirement, whereas the remaining two (Boudouaia et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0013); Li, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0067)) only partially satisfied the criteria. Two additional studies (Guo, Pan, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0037); Hudon et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0043)) had sample sizes between 101 and 200 participants; one (Guo, Pan, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0037)) partially met the threshold, while the other (Hudon et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0043)) did not meet it. Only one study (Meyer et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0084)) included more than 200 participants, but it also failed to meet the minimum effect size requirement. Overall, in the context of the externalization mode, three studies (Kortemeyer, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0056); Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076); Ouyang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0095)) fully met the minimum required effect size, four studies (Boudouaia et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0013); Guo, Pan, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0037); Li, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0067); Wiboolyasarin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0127)) partially met the criteria and three studies (Hudon et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0043); Meyer et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0084); Naik et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0088)) did not meet the threshold.
#### Internalization
For the internalization mode (ie, low AI automation and high human agency), parametric statistical tests were used in the majority of studies, appearing in 17 cases, while non-parametric tests were employed in 11 studies. Among parametric tests, the independent samples \*t\*-test was the most frequently used, cited in 10 sources (Chandrasekera et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0014); Dos Santos & Cury, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0025); Essel et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0027); Kazemitabaar, Chow, et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0051); Liu et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0073); Liu & Wang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0075); Nguyen et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0091); Sun et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0115); Wang et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0123); Wu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0129)). Paired samples \*t\*-tests were referenced in four sources (Liu, Li, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0072); Liu & Wang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0075); Tsai et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0118); Wang et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0123)). ANOVA and ANCOVA were each used five times across different studies, with ANOVA appearing in sources (Chen & Chang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0015); Dos Santos & Cury, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0025); Hu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0041); Huang & Mizumoto, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0042); Tai & Chen, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0117)) and ANCOVA in (Chen & Chang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0015); Darmawansah et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0021); Essel et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0027); Hu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0041); Wu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0129); Yilmaz & Yilmaz, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0136)). MANOVA and MANCOVA were less common, cited in two (Liu et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0073); Naamati-Schneider & Alt, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0087)) and one (Naamati-Schneider & Alt, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0087)) sources, respectively. Of the 17 studies that used parametric statistical tests, half explicitly reported conducting assumption checks to ensure the conditions for using parametric methods were satisfied, while the other half did not clearly state whether such tests were performed. For non-parametric tests, the chi-square test was applied once (Dos Santos & Cury, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0025)), while the Mann–Whitney \*U\*-test was more prominent, referenced in seven studies (Kavadella et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0049); Kazemitabaar, Chow, et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0051); Kumar, Musabirov, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0058); Kumar, Xiao, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0059); Li et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0069); Ng et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0090); Zhu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0138)). The Wilcoxon signed-rank test appeared in seven sources as well (Guo, Zhang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0038); Kumar, Musabirov, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0058); Li et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0069); Ng et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0090); Niloy et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0093); Tsai et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0118); Zhu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0138)), and the Kruskal–Wallis test was cited once (Kumar, Musabirov, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0058)).
Among the seven studies with small sample sizes (\*N\* ≤ 50), five partially met the criterion (Guo, Zhang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0038); Li et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0069); Liu, Li, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0072); Wang et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0123); Yilmaz & Yilmaz, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0136)) and two did not meet it (Chandrasekera et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0014); Nguyen et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0091)). Of the 12 studies with medium sample sizes (51 < \*N\* ≤ 100), five satisfied the minimum effect size requirement (Darmawansah et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0021); Hu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0041); Ng et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0090); Tai & Chen, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0117); Wu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0129)), another five were partially compliant (Huang & Mizumoto, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0042); Kazemitabaar, Chow, et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0051); Liu et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0073); Sun et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0115); Tsai et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0118)), and two failed to meet the criterion (Kavadella et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0049); Liu & Wang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0075)). In the category with sample sizes between 101 and 200, only two studies partially met the criterion (Essel et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0027); Zhu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0138)). For the four studies with the largest sample sizes (\*N\* > 200), two fully met the minimum effect size requirement (Kumar, Musabirov, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0058); Niloy et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0093)) and two partially met it (Chen & Chang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0015); Naamati-Schneider & Alt, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0087)); none failed to meet the threshold. Overall, in the context of the Internalization mode, seven studies (Darmawansah et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0021); Hu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0041); Kumar, Musabirov, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0058); Ng et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0090); Niloy et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0093); Tai & Chen, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0117); Wu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0129)) fully met the minimum required effect size, 14 studies (Chen & Chang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0015); Guo, Zhang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0038); Huang & Mizumoto, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0042); Kazemitabaar, Chow, et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0051); Kumar, Musabirov, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0058); Li et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0069); Liu et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0073); Liu, Li, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0072); Naamati-Schneider & Alt, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0087); Niloy et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0093); Sun et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0115); Tsai et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0118); Wang et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0123); Yilmaz & Yilmaz, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0136)) partially met the criteria and only four studies (Chandrasekera et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0014); Kavadella et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0049); Liu & Wang, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0075); Nguyen et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0091)) did not meet the threshold.
#### Hybrid intelligence
For hybrid intelligence mode (ie, high AI automation and high human agency), parametric statistical tests were used in the majority of studies, appearing in eight, while non-parametric tests were employed in only one study. Among the parametric methods, ANCOVA was the most frequently used, cited in five studies (Wu et al., [n.d.](#bjet70055-bib-0128); Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034); Lee, Chen, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0064); Lin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0071); Song & Song, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)), followed by independent samples \*t\*-tests in four studies (Ba et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0006); Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034); Lin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0071); Song & Song, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)). Hierarchical linear modelling and ordinary least squares regression were each reported in one study (Darban, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0020)), and ANOVA was used in a single study (Peng et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)). Of the eight studies using parametric tests, seven explicitly reported conducting assumption checks, while one study did not clearly indicate whether such checks were performed. The only non-parametric method reported was the chi-square test, used in a single study (Ba et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0006)).
Among the four studies with small sample sizes (\*N\* ≤ 50), two fully met the minimum effect size requirements (Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034); Peng et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)), while the other two were partially satisfied (Wu et al., [n.d.](#bjet70055-bib-0128); Song & Song, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)). Of the three studies with medium-sized samples (51 < \*N\* ≤ 100), two only partially met the criterion (Lee, Chen, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0064); Lin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0071)) and one did not meet it (Ba et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0006)). No studies were identified within the 101 < \*N ≤\* 200 range. Only one study (Darban, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0020)) with large sample sizes (\*N\* > 200) partially met the minimum effect size requirement. Overall, in the context of the hybrid intelligence mode, only two studies (Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034); Peng et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100)) fully met the minimum required effect size, five studies (Wu et al., [n.d.](#bjet70055-bib-0128); Darban, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0020); Lee, Chen, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0064); Lin et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0071); Song & Song, [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0113)) partially met the criteria and only one study (Ba et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0006)) did not meet the threshold.
#### Overview of the 12 high-effect studies
Among the 12 studies that met or exceeded the minimum required effect size threshold listed in Table [12](#bjet70055-tbl-0012), most (10 of 12) involved students as participants, whereas the remaining two focused on educators. The internalization mode of AI use was found to be the most common, appearing in seven of the studies. In terms of educational context, eight studies were conducted in higher education and five in K–12 settings. The most frequently examined educational tasks included educational feedback and dialogue (eight studies), question answering (six studies) and written assessment assistance (five studies). From a methodological perspective, the majority of studies applied parametric statistical tests such as independent samples \*t\*-tests, ANOVA and ANCOVA to compare experimental and control groups. To strengthen the robustness of the analyses, researchers also employed non-parametric methods including the Wilcoxon signed-rank test, the Mann–Whitney \*U\*-test and the Kruskal–Wallis test.

## DISCUSSION
In this systematic review, we explored the human–AI interaction modes utilized in empirical studies involving GenAI in educational contexts. Guided by the AIED-HCD conceptual framework (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)) that positions interaction modes along the dimensions of human control and AI automation, our analysis established several unique contributions to the understanding of how GenAI is currently being integrated into education research and revealed critical gaps surrounding methodological rigour and statistical reporting practices.
### Current trends and implications of human–AI interaction modes
Our results reveal a notable dominance of the \*Internalization\* mode, with most studies (\*n\* = 34) positioning GenAI primarily as an informational support or cognitive scaffold rather than as a fully integrated, intelligent collaborator. This approach aligns with prior theoretical arguments emphasizing human agency, autonomy and control within AI-supported learning environments (Alfredo et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0002); Raees et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0104)). The internalization mode enables learners to engage thoughtfully by seeking AI-driven advice and recommendations, thereby fostering skills development and personalized reflection processes, as suggested by prior research (Nikolic et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0092); Yan, Martinez-Maldonado, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0132)).
However, the widespread presence of this mode also indicates that the vast majority of current empirical studies still situate GenAI tools within high human-control conditions, while research exploring high AI-automation modes in practical educational settings remains noticeably limited. This distribution may reflect a cautious orientation in current research and practice, where greater emphasis is placed on maintaining human agency and oversight rather than on relying extensively on automated collaboration.
One potential reason for this dominance may lie in the current difficulty of implementing high levels of externalization in authentic educational practices. At the same time, considering that prior research in the field of self-regulated learning has consistently shown that students often lack strong metacognitive abilities (Molenaar, [2022](#bjet70055-bib-0085)), whether learners can truly take effective control under the Internalization mode remains a question that requires further empirical investigation. While this conservative mode maintains human-centric values and safeguards learners and educators against potential algorithmic biases or unexpected AI behaviours, we anticipate an increase in innovative studies exploring more advanced interaction modes as GenAI technologies mature and stakeholders become more comfortable integrating higher automation into practice.
The \*Externalization\* approach, characterized by low-human control alongside high-AI automation, was noticeably less frequently seen (\*n\* = 11). The use of externalization mostly targeted domains with clearly defined task structures, such as programming, language learning and STEM education. This finding resonates well with recent recommendations by Vaccaro et al. ([2024](#bjet70055-bib-0120)) and Vaccaro and Waldo ([2019](#bjet70055-bib-0121)), who emphasized the importance of thoroughly assessing the respective strengths of humans and AI and making appropriate task allocations before delegating tasks to GenAI, in order to mitigate potential risks. Such tasks entail high interpretive diversity and contextual nuances that automation alone may inadequately capture. However, as GenAI and its capacity for contextual understanding continue to improve, additional research could evaluate whether subsequent advancements can reliably extend externalization-based interaction modes safely and effectively into more nuanced, social or context-dependent educational tasks. It is important to recognize that the externalization mode should not be viewed as an inferior form of human–AI interaction, but rather as a context-dependent instructional design choice. For beginners or learners who are not yet familiar with a topic, externalizing part of the learning or cognitive tasks to GenAI allows the system to automatically perform or guide specific learning processes, which can sometimes be more effective than approaches that require active participation in complex knowledge co-construction or collaborative design (Jin et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0046); Martin et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0081); Suh et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0114)). Requiring novice learners to engage in demanding cooperative exploration may lead to cognitive overload and negatively affect their learning experience (Anderl et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0003)). Therefore, the value of the externalization mode lies in enhancing instructional effectiveness through AI-supported regulation under high levels of automation, particularly by reducing cognitive load, providing immediate feedback and ensuring smooth task progression. While this mode may be especially suitable for novice or entry-level learners, its contribution to deeper conceptual understanding may be limited. As learners' competencies and understanding of AI develop, instructional control may gradually shift towards co-regulation and, eventually, towards more advanced forms of human–AI interaction, supporting learners' progressive assumption of self-regulatory responsibility (Molenaar, [2022](#bjet70055-bib-0085)).
One of the most intriguing findings resulting from our exploration is the nascent emergence of hybrid intelligence mode (\*n\* = 9), representing close dynamic collaboration between human stakeholders and GenAI, each actively contributing their unique strengths in real-time and iterative exchanges. While this category of research remains in an exploratory stage, partly because most existing studies were published between 2023 and 2024 and partly because current GenAI tools have not yet fully achieved real time, tightly coupled collaboration with humans (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)), early results have shown promising trends, such as significant improvements in learner engagement, teacher efficiency and the quality of personalized teaching practices (Barany et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0010)).
Prior systematic reviews analysing GenAI's educational use have typically failed to differentiate modes of interaction explicitly or define their implications comprehensively, often offering overly general conclusions about GenAI effectiveness and use cases (Guo, Zheng, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0039); Nikolic et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0092); Zhang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0137)).
Although the hybrid intelligence mode shows great promise in supporting higher order thinking and personalized learning (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018)), its implementation in practical educational settings still faces several challenges. These include relatively high technical challenges in building such AI systems and the demanding cognitive and self-regulatory requirements they place on users. For example, designing an AI tutor capable of engaging with students in open-ended problem solving requires the system to achieve deep integration of natural language understanding, real-time learning analytics and pedagogical reasoning, while also demanding that learners possess strong cognitive and metacognitive regulation skills to maintain active intellectual engagement and learning agency throughout the collaboration. Therefore, in practical teaching contexts, the adoption of the hybrid intelligence model must involve thoughtful instructional design, clearly defined learning objectives, and appropriate guidance and support to ensure its educational benefits are fully realized.
Based on the patterns identified in this systematic review, the findings suggest that no single form of human–AI interaction can be regarded as universally ideal. It needs to adapt to the nature of the task, learner characteristics, teacher expertise and the specific ways in which the tools are deployed. For tasks that are outcome-oriented, such as auto-grading or written correction, where the primary goal is to improve efficiency and provide immediate support, the externalization mode can offer higher effectiveness (Korthals et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0057); Meyer et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0084)). In contrast, for learning activities that require creativity, critical reflection or collaborative knowledge building, the hybrid intelligence mode may better facilitate deep learning and the development of higher order thinking (Cukurova, [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0018); Kong et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0055); Wei et al., [2025](#bjet70055-bib-0124)). Future research and practice should avoid assuming that the ideal state of human–AI interaction simply involves more intensive interaction or full automation. As our findings suggest, the effectiveness and outcomes of different interaction modes vary across educational goals and contexts, reflecting a certain level of complexity in their functioning. Instead, they should begin with educational goals and learner needs to establish an evidence-based framework for selecting appropriate interaction modes. Such a framework should specify the conditions for switching between modes, define appropriate entry requirements and support mechanisms for different groups of learners and provide teachers with replicable templates and evaluation tools. By maintaining a dynamic balance between efficiency and depth, educators and researchers can more effectively harness the potential of human–AI interaction and maximize the educational value of GenAI.
### Limitations in demonstrating statistical rigour and methodological concerns
A pivotal observation from our sensitivity analysis is the widespread lack of statistical rigour and transparency in reporting across GenAI-empirical literature. While prior reviews have highlighted general issues of methodological robustness in educational technology research (Cohen, [2013](#bjet70055-bib-0016); Frias-Navarro et al., [2020](#bjet70055-bib-0032); Lakens, [2022](#bjet70055-bib-0061); Vaccaro et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0120)), our mode-specific analysis reveals new, actionable insights about where these limitations are most acute and how they manifest across different forms of human–AI interaction. Specifically, if we disregard the distinctions between human–AI interaction modes and simply aggregate all studies, only a modest fraction of the reviewed literature fully met the minimum effect size requirements for adequate statistical power. Specifically, out of the total studies included, just 12 (across all modes) reported sample sizes and effect sizes that satisfied the conventional threshold (power ≥ 0.80). This means that, in the absence of careful attention to interaction mode or sample size, the majority of GenAI studies in education may be drawing conclusions from statistically underpowered analyses. Such a pattern raises concerns about the reliability and generalizability of the field's current empirical insights, echoing broader concerns about reproducibility and interpretive clarity in educational research (Frias-Navarro et al., [2020](#bjet70055-bib-0032); Yan, Greiff, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0131)).
The internalization mode, which was the most prevalent (\*n\* = 34), also had the largest absolute number of studies (7) that fully met the minimum effect size requirement (Darmawansah et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0021); Hu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0041); Kumar, Musabirov, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0058); Ng et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0090); Niloy et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0093); Tai & Chen, [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0117); Wu et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0129)). This suggests that, while not perfect, research in this mode was relatively more likely to yield statistically robust findings. Although the number of statistically adequate studies is higher in the internalization mode, this likely reflects the relatively more established nature of research designs and sample recruitment in this category, where human agency is emphasized and GenAI functions clearly as a cognitive scaffold. In contrast, the externalization mode (\*n\* = 13) and the hybrid intelligence mode (\*n\* = 9) had fewer statistically adequate studies, with only two studies meeting the criteria in each [Externalization: (Liu, Hwang, et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0076); Ouyang et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0095)); Hybrid Intelligence: (Ghafouri et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0034); Peng et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0100))]. However, the compliance rates across the three modes are quite similar, suggesting that the observed differences are primarily due to unequal sample sizes rather than any substantial difference in methodological quality. This is particularly concerning for hybrid intelligence, where the complexity of tightly coupled human–AI collaboration may demand even greater methodological rigour to disentangle the contributions of each agent and to ensure findings are not artefacts of small or unrepresentative samples.
These disparities have important implications. Although the internalization mode had a larger absolute number of statistically adequate studies, this should not be interpreted as evidence of greater methodological rigour since the compliance rates are similar across the three modes. Rather, this difference more likely reflects the more established research designs and participant recruitment practices typical of Internalization studies. By contrast, the limited number of statistically adequate studies in the externalization and hybrid intelligence modes suggests that current claims about the effectiveness of fully automated or deeply collaborative GenAI systems should be interpreted with caution. Overall, these findings point to a broader methodological gap that extends beyond any single interaction mode.
This gap underscores the urgent need for future research in these areas to prioritize robust sample size calculations, transparent reporting of statistical assumptions and rigorous methodological design. Moreover, our analysis of reporting practices revealed that, across all modes, a substantial number of studies failed to clearly document whether assumption checks for parametric tests were conducted. This lack of transparency further undermines confidence in the reported findings and complicates efforts to synthesize evidence across studies (Cohen, [2013](#bjet70055-bib-0016); Lakens, [2022](#bjet70055-bib-0061)). Given that parametric tests are sensitive to violations of assumptions such as normality and homogeneity of variance, failure to report or conduct these checks can lead to inflated Type I or Type II error rates, further compromising the reliability of conclusions.
### Recommended pathways for future research and practice
Based on our analyses and systematic comparisons of the diverse GenAI human–AI interaction modes identified, we provide three main recommendations for future research and practice. First, considering the dominant use of the internalization mode and the comparatively limited engagement with tightly integrated hybrid intelligence approaches, educational institutions and research organizations should actively explore ways to pragmatically increase AI automation levels. This would enable the integration of human and AI strengths to enhance cognition, support decision-making and achieve more efficient cooperation than either could alone, while maintaining human control, carefully managing risks and ensuring responsible applications. Experimental hybrid intelligence interactions experimenting with state-of-the-art GenAI models (eg, GPT-4-turbo, GPT-4.5, Gemini 2.5) in controlled educational experiments should be prioritized, offering rich opportunities to identify intensity and types of human–AI interaction most beneficial for specific educational goals and constraints (Kasneci et al., [2023](#bjet70055-bib-0048); Kazemitabaar, Hou, et al., [2023c](#bjet70055-bib-0052)). Second, new generations of GenAI technologies frequently emerge, continually reshaping capacity limits and effective interaction boundaries. Continued awareness of the newest AI advances and careful verification of their suitability for different interaction modes could further optimize educational user experiences, expand hybrid intelligence opportunities and deliver systematically measurable improvements and efficiencies to the educational landscape (Barany et al., [2024](#bjet70055-bib-0010)). Third, our sensitivity analyses demonstrate significant room for improvement in educational research transparency, scientific rigour and reporting practices concerning GenAI implementations. Future studies must present explicit assumptions driving parametric statistics, justify the choice and reporting of effect sizes and ensure adequately computed, adequately powered sample sizes, thereby maximizing interpretive relevance, clarity and empirical reliability of insights delivered to future educators and education policymakers alike.
### Limitations
This systematic literature review has several limitations. Although we adopted a systematic and comprehensive search strategy, limitations in keyword selection, database coverage and the inclusion of only English language publications may have led to the omission of relevant studies. Some rapidly emerging work may not have been included in the analysis. Moreover, although the current study identified and analysed different human–AI interaction modes, it may still present certain limitations in differentiating between the concepts of ‘learning’ and ‘performance’, which warrants further investigation in future research. Despite these limitations, this review aims to analyse the current patterns of human and AI interaction and provide a useful reference for future empirical research that is more systematic and comprehensive.

### Download Citation
If you have the appropriate software installed, you can download article citation data to the citation manager of your choice.
Simply select your manager software from the list below and click on download.
Select formatPlain textRIS (ProCite, Reference Manager)EndNoteBibTeXMedlarsRefWorks

Tips on downloading citation
This feature enables you to download the bibliographic information (also called citation data, header data, or metadata) for the articles on our site.
### Citation manager file format
Use the dropdown list to choose how to format the bibliographic data you're harvesting. Several citation manager formats are available, including EndNote and BibTex. You can then copy the formatted citation (as displayed) or download it as file, to your device. If the RefWorks format is chosen, the 'Download' button will be replaced with an option to directly export to RefWorks
